    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_120990977" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/120990977" />
    <a title="Kristina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4272284-kristina"><img alt="Kristina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584248335p2/4272284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/120990977?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristina" href="/user/show/4272284-kristina">Kristina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4272284-kristina?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4272284-kristina?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4272284-kristina?shelf=loved-em">loved-em</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4272284-kristina?shelf=religion-and-philosophy">religion-and-philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4272284-kristina?shelf=my-book">my-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4272284-kristina?shelf=library-book">library-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4272284-kristina?shelf=social-sciences">social-sciences</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer120990977" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6795602006420841233"><blockquote>O, what a world of unseen visions and heard silences, this insubstantial country of the mind! What ineffable essences, these touchless rememberings and unshowable reveries! And the privacy of it all! A secret theater of speechless monologues and prevenient counsel, an invisible mansion of all moods, musings, and mysteries, an infinite resort of disappointment and discoveries. A whole kingdom where each of us reigns exclusively alone, questioning what we will, commanding what we can. A hidden her</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText6795602006420841233" style="display:none"><blockquote>O, what a world of unseen visions and heard silences, this insubstantial country of the mind! What ineffable essences, these touchless rememberings and unshowable reveries! And the privacy of it all! A secret theater of speechless monologues and prevenient counsel, an invisible mansion of all moods, musings, and mysteries, an infinite resort of disappointment and discoveries. A whole kingdom where each of us reigns exclusively alone, questioning what we will, commanding what we can. A hidden hermitage where we may study out the troubled book of what we have done and yet may do. An introcosm that is more myself than anything I can find in a mirror. This consciousness that is myself of selves, that is everything, and yet nothing at all—what is it?<br />	And where did it come from?<br />	And why?</blockquote><br /><br />So begins Julian Jaynes’s eloquently written and fascinating book <i>The Origin of Consciousness in the Breakdown of the Bicameral Mind</i>. Very few books delight and intrigue me the way this one has. I was (and still am) blown away by the theory of consciousness that Jaynes presents, and its implications regarding mankind’s history and future. Just <i>wow</i>. <br /><br />Jaynes disagrees with the idea that consciousness is a product of animal evolution, but instead cites scientific studies, archaeological evidence and a close examination of ancient texts such as the <i>Illiad</i> and the Old Testament to offer an alternative theory of consciousness: it was a gradual, learned response to catastrophic events of 3000 years ago. Ancient men were not conscious and did not think the way we do. They could not introspect nor think for themselves. Instead they relied upon auditory hallucinations which were produced by the brain’s right hemisphere. These voices were considered gods who spoke directly to people and told them what to do in times of stress or uncommon, unusual situations. This ancient mentality is called the bicameral mind. Jaynes lays out the evidence to support his theory in this 450+ page book (which he charmingly refers to as “this essay”) by first exploring what consciousness is <i>not</i> and the origin of civilization, then turning to in depth examinations of archaeological records. The last section in the book deals with the vestiges of bicamerality in the modern age. <br /><br />I’m not really going to pick apart the book and discuss what impressed me the most because I can’t. The whole book is absolutely fascinating and presented me with a completely new perspective on history, religion, science, and psychology. I have a million post-it notes stuck to the pages because on almost every page is something that made me have a “holy crap!” moment. This is one of those books that I will have to read a couple of times to truly take it all in. Which is probably why this review will not do this mind-blowing book justice—I finished this book almost a week ago and I’m still thinking about it. While Book I (“The Mind of Men”) defines consciousness and explores the evidence of bicamerality, Book II (“The Witness of History”) discusses the beginning of consciousness and the causes of the breakdown of the bicameral mind. Specific citations from the Old Testament which show mankind grasping for answers from the now (mostly) absent gods make me want to go buy an excellent (linguistically accurate) translation of the Old Testament and read it to find evidence myself of this breakdown of the bicameral mind. <br /><br />After looking over historical events of the past 3000+ years, one of Jaynes’s conclusions is: humans are still searching for authorization, for that voice of absolute truth, for the gods. Religion and science, Jaynes argues, have only recently been portrayed as enemies. However, both are ways of searching for divine certainties: “If we would understand the Scientific Revolution correctly, we should always remember that its most powerful impetus was the unremitting search for hidden divinity…without this religious motivation, science would have been mere technology, limping along on economic necessity…The real chasm was between the political authority of the church and the individual authority of experience. And the real question was whether we are to find our lost authorization through an apostolic succession from ancient prophets who heard divine voices, or through searching the heavens of our own experience right now in the objective world without any priestly intercession” (435). When our search for god/divine truth is frustrated, we turn to substitutions: astrology, meditation, pseudosciences (scientology and UFOs), drugs, among other practices. Modern science, he says, can also be susceptible to quasi-religious tendencies (that is, portraying itself as the divine/absolute truth). He calls these “scientisms” as and they possess these characteristics: a rational splendor that explains everything, a charismatic leader or succession of leaders who are highly visible and beyond criticism, a series of canonical texts which are somehow also outside normal scientific scrutiny, certain gestures of idea and rituals of interpretation and a requirement of total commitment. Nineteenth century Medical Materialism and Marxism are two examples he cites as scientisms. (If he were writing this book now, he'd include Scientology in that list. It fits perfectly.)<br /><br /><i>The Origin of Consciousness in the Breakdown of the Bicameral Mind</i> was published in 1976, and I am amazed that it is not more mainstream. I feel as if high school and college cheated me by not throwing this book at me and making me read it. It is controversial (still) because if flies in the face of accepted theories and truisms. However, Jaynes writes so persuasively and his evidence is so astounding that one has to—at the very least—entertain the possibility that his theory is correct. Even if, in the end, the idea of consciousness as a learned behavior is rejected, it’s still necessary to re-examine the idea of consciousness and historical motivations. And it’s never a bad idea to re-examine your mostly closely held beliefs. <br /><blockquote>The very notion of truth is a culturally given direction, a part of the pervasive nostalgia for an earlier certainty. The very idea of a universal stability, an eternal firmness of principle <i>out there</i> that can be sought for through the world as might an Arthurian knight for the Grail, is, in the morphology of history, a direct outgrowth of the search for lost gods in the first two millennia after the decline of the bicameral mind. What was then an augury for direction of action among the ruins of an archaic mentality is now the search for an innocence of certainty among the mythologies of facts (446).</blockquote><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6795602006420841233" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_498851799" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/498851799" />
    <a title="Keith Swenson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3733820-keith-swenson"><img alt="Keith Swenson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1336407708p2/3733820.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/498851799?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Keith Swenson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Keith Swenson" href="/user/show/3733820-keith-swenson">Keith Swenson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer498851799" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer639061995090306270">I must fall back on description of the book given by someone else: it is "either complete rubbish or the work of a consummate genius ... nothing in between."<br/><br/>Gave the book 4 stars because it is one of those books that really makes you think about everything. <br/><br/>What Jaynes does do is to look at the periods of history and identify a pattern of psychological differences over time by analyzing the writing left to us by those people. He sees a rather distinct change happen about 1000 BCE in the middle e</span>
  <span id="freeText639061995090306270" style="display:none">I must fall back on description of the book given by someone else: it is "either complete rubbish or the work of a consummate genius ... nothing in between."<br /><br />Gave the book 4 stars because it is one of those books that really makes you think about everything. <br /><br />What Jaynes does do is to look at the periods of history and identify a pattern of psychological differences over time by analyzing the writing left to us by those people. He sees a rather distinct change happen about 1000 BCE in the middle east (other times in other parts of the world). He sees that trend as part of a cultural-psychological arc that extends across history in several stages: the "Enlightenment" as a more recent development in this trend.<br /><br />The idea is so wacky: that the pyramids were built by unconscious people; that consciousness is a behavior that you learn from others in the your culture. I would imagine an unconscious person as wandering around as if in a dream. Jaynes' definition of consciousness might better be termed 'meta-consciousness': being aware that you are aware. The biggest flaw in the book is to call this property "consciousness" because it grates against the pop-psychology notion of consciousness. Had he invented a word (e.g. "grok") the book might be easier to discuss. Even with that, I can't say that I "believe" or even "agree" with his central thesis -- it is just too sketchy.<br /><br />If you can get past this limitation, there are undeniable truths in the book that explain things that few have tried to explain. Why did so many major cultures trace their roots back to around 600BC? Why did the Oracle at Delphi persist as an institution for 1000 years with the region's smartest people visiting it regularly. There are so many beginnings at that time period that it is only natural to ask: what really changed at that time? And what is it that has really changed since then? I keep thinking of Monte Python's "Life of Brian" where they depict a time when prophets and miracle workers were a daily occurrence. <br /><br />There is no doubt that members of our modern cultures think about the world in a vastly different way than people did a few thousand years ago. Jaynes attempts to plot the course of that change, and show evidence from writing at different times in history. There is certainly no question that people today think about the world the way we do because we learned it from our culture. How then does a culture change from believing in demons and gods in everything around, to a more rational view of the world? It probably didn't happened the way that Jaynes describes it, but then there still is always a chance that he is right. There is at least a germ of truth in what he says.<br /><br />It is not an easy read, and draws on a hefty understanding of ancient history. Yet, it is one of those "mind blowing" books that may change your view of some things forever. I feel certain, that some future philosopher will work out actual course of such social/psychological development, and that work will no doubt cite Julian Jaynes as one of the initial sparks of innovation in that direction.</span>
  <a data-text-id="639061995090306270" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('xR++vAJc6ab+XpzL3nQqVl5wDILxb+P4fJb6+pqQNA5f7fZx5f43/o+rGL0ARdi2b9hZgQnARrhwdkf33CA66w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

The difference between right &amp; left hemispheres has been puzzled over for centuries. In a book of unprecedented scope, McGilchrist draws on a vast body of recent brain research, illustrated with case histories, to reveal that the difference is profound—not just this or that function, but two whole, coherent, but incompatible ways of experiencing the world. The left hemisphere is detail oriented, prefers mechanisms to living things &amp; is inclined to self-interest. The right hemisphere has greater breadth, flexibility &amp; generosity. This division helps explain the origins of music &amp; language, &amp; casts new light on the history of philosophy, as well as on some mental illnesses. The 2nd part of the book takes a journey thru the history of Western culture, illustrating the tension between these two worlds as revealed in the thought &amp; belief of thinkers &amp; artists, from Aeschylus to Magritte. He argues that, despite its inferior grasp of reality, the left hemisphere is increasingly taking precedence in the modern world, with potentially disastrous consequences.\nList of       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_13557108'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13557108-consciousness?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Consciousness: Confessions of a Romantic Reductionist<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/72158.Christof_Koch?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Christof Koch<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.11 avg rating &mdash; 981 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2012\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer13293479064726963596\">In which a scientist searches for an empirical explanation for phenomenal experience, spurred by his instinctual belief that life is meaningful.\n\nWhat links conscious experience of pain, joy, color, and smell to bioelectrical activity in the brain? H<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText13293479064726963596\" style=\"display:none\">In which a scientist searches for an empirical explanation for phenomenal experience, spurred by his instinctual belief that life is meaningful.\n\nWhat links conscious experience of pain, joy, color, and smell to bioelectrical activity in the brain? How can anything physical give rise to nonphysical, subjective, conscious states? Christof Koch has devoted much of his career to bridging the seemingly unbridgeable gap between the physics of the brain and phenomenal experience. This engaging book--part scientific overview, part memoir, part futurist speculation--describes Koch\'s search for an empirical explanation for consciousness. Koch recounts not only the birth of the modern science of consciousness but also the subterranean motivation for his quest--his instinctual (if &quot;romantic&quot;) belief that life is meaningful.\n\nKoch describes his own groundbreaking work with Francis Crick in the 1990s and 2000s and the gradual emergence of consciousness (once considered a &quot;fringy&quot; subject) as a legitimate topic for scientific investigation. Present at this paradigm shift were Koch and a handful of colleagues, including Ned Block, David Chalmers, Stanislas Dehaene, Giulio Tononi, Wolf Singer, and others. Aiding and abetting it were new techniques to listen in on the activity of individual nerve cells, clinical studies, and brain-imaging technologies that allowed safe and noninvasive study of the human brain in action.\n\nKoch gives us stories from the front lines of modern research into the neurobiology of consciousness as well as his own reflections on a variety of topics, including the distinction between attention and awareness, the unconscious, how neurons respond to Homer Simpson, the physics and biology of free will, dogs, Der Ring des Nibelungen, sentient machines, the loss of his belief in a personal God, and sadness. All of them are signposts in the pursuit of his life\'s work--to uncover the roots of consciousness.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"13293479064726963596\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; Why do well-intentioned plans for improving the human condition go tragically awry?In this wide-ranging and original book, James C. Scott analyzes failed cases of large-scale authoritarian plans in a variety of fields. Centrally managed social plans misfire, Scott argues, when they impose schematic visions that do violence to complex interdependencies that are not—and cannot—be fully understood. Further, the success of designs for social organization depends upon the recognition that local, practical knowledge is as important as formal, epistemic knowledge. The author builds a persuasive case against &quot;development theory&quot; and imperialistic state planning that disregards the values, desires, and objections of its subjects. He identifies and discusses four conditions common to all planning disasters: administrative ordering of nature and society by the state; a &quot;high-modernist ideology&quot; that places confidence in the ability of science to improve every aspect of human life; a willingness to use authoritarian state power to effect large- scale interventions; and a prostrate civil society that cannot If we started off with rudimentary utterances on the level of &quot;man throw spear,&quot; how did we end up with sophisticated grammars, enormous vocabularies, and intricately nuanced degrees of meaning?\n\nDrawing on recent groundbreaking discoveries in modern linguistics, Deutscher exposes the elusive forces of creation at work in human communication, giving us fresh insight into how language emerges, evolves, and decays. He traces the evolution of linguistic complexity from an early &quot;Me Tarzan&quot; stage to such elaborate single-word constructions as the Turkish sehirlilestiremediklerimizdensiniz (&quot;you are one of those whom we couldn\'t turn into a town dweller&quot;). Arguing that destruction and creation in language are intimately entwined, Deutscher shows how these processes are continuously in operation, generating new words, new structures, and new meanings.\n\nAs entertaining as it is erudite, The Unfolding of Language moves nimbly from ancient Babylonian to American idiom, from the central role of metaphor to the staggering triumph of design that is the Semitic verb, to tell the dramatic story and explain the genius behind a uniquely human faculty.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"8619318720319523342\" href=\"#\" On the one hand, we struggle to survive on our own in the wild, often unable to solve basic problems, like obtaining food, building shelters, or avoiding predators. On the other hand, human groups have produced innovative technologies, sophisticated languages, and complex institutions that have permitted us to successfully expand into environments across the globe. What has enabled us to dominate such a vast range of environments, more than any other species? As this book shows, the secret of our success lies not in our innate intelligence, but in our collective brains--in the ability of human groups to socially interconnect and learn from one another.\n\nDrawing insights from lost European explorers, clever chimpanzees, hunter-gatherers, neuroscientists, ancient bones, and the human genome, Joseph Henrich demonstrates how our collective brains have propelled our species\' genetic evolution and shaped our biology. Our early capacities for learning from others produced many innovations, such as fire, cooking, water containers, plant knowledge, and projectile weapons, which in turn drove the expansion of our brains and altered our physiology, anatomy, and psychology in crucial ways. Further on, some collective brains generated and recombined powerful concepts, such as the lever, wheel, screw, and writing. Henrich shows how our genetics and biology are inextricably interwoven with cultural evolution, and that this particular culture-gene interaction has propelled our species on an extraordinary evolutionary trajectory.\n\nTracking clues from our ancient past to the present, &quot;The Secret of Our Success&quot; explores how our cultural and social natures produce a collective intelligence He called it \"the fruit of near<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText15821632284782860033\" style=\"display:none\">One of the most important of Jung\'s longer works, and probably the most famous of his books, Psychological Types appeared in German in 1921 after a &quot;fallow period&quot; of eight years during which Jung had published little. He called it &quot;the fruit of nearly twenty years\' work in the domain of practical psychology,&quot; and in his autobiography he wrote: &quot;This work sprang originally from my need to define the ways in which my outlook differed from Freud\'s and Adler\'s. In attempting to answer this question, I came across the problem of types; for it is one\'s psychological type which from the outset determines and limits a person\'s judgment. My book, therefore, was an effort to deal with the relationship of the individual to the world, to people and things. It discussed the various aspects of consciousness, the various attitudes the conscious mind might take toward the world, and thus constitutes a psychology of consciousness regarded from what might be called a clinical angle.&quot; In expounding his system of personality types Jung relied not so much on formal case data as on the countless impressions and experiences derived from the treatment of nervous illnesses, from intercourse with people of all social levels, &quot;friend and foe alike,&quot; and from an analysis of his own psychological nature. The book is rich in material drawn from literature, aesthetics, religion, and philosophy.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/12810.Julian_Jaynes">About Julian Jaynes</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/12810.Julian_Jaynes"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1297550394p3/12810.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/12810.Julian_Jaynes">Julian Jaynes
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
99
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6695161521244657369">Julian Jaynes was an American psychologist, best known for his book The Origin of Consciousness in the Breakdown of the Bicameral Mind (1976), in which he argued that ancient peoples were not conscious.<br/>Jaynes defines "consciousness" more narrowly than some philosophers. Jaynes' definition of consciousness is synonymous with what philosophers now call "meta-consciousness" or "meta-awareness" i.e. a</span>
  <span id="freeText6695161521244657369" style="display:none">Julian Jaynes was an American psychologist, best known for his book The Origin of Consciousness in the Breakdown of the Bicameral Mind (1976), in which he argued that ancient peoples were not conscious.<br />Jaynes defines "consciousness" more narrowly than some philosophers. Jaynes' definition of consciousness is synonymous with what philosophers now call "meta-consciousness" or "meta-awareness" i.e. awareness of awareness, thoughts about thinking, desires about desires, beliefs about beliefs. This form of reflection is also distinct from the kinds of "deliberations" seen in other higher animals such as crows insofar as Jaynesian consciousness is dependent on linguistic cognition.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6695161521244657369" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1311139-the-origin-of-consciousness-in-the-breakdown-of-the-bicameral-mind">Quotes from The Origin of Con...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;O, what a world of unseen visions and heard silences, this insubstantial country of the mind! What ineffable essences, these touchless rememberings and unshowable reveries! And the privacy of it all! A secret theater of speechless monologue and prevenient counsel, an invisible mansion of all moods, musings, and mysteries, an infinite resort of disappointments and discoveries. A whole kingdom where each of us reigns reclusively alone, questioning what we will, commanding what we can. A hidden hermitage where we may study out the troubled book of what we have done and yet may do. An introcosm that is more myself than anything I can find in a mirror. This consciousness that is myself of selves, that is everything, and yet is nothing at all - what is it?<br />And where did it come from?<br />And why?&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/364997-o-what-a-world-of-unseen-visions-and-heard-silences">33 likes</a>

          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Our sense of justice depends on our sense of time. Justice is a phenomenon only of consciousness, because time spread out in a spatial succession is its very essence. And this is possible only in a spatial metaphor of time.&rdquo;</span>
